INTRODUCTION  
TO  
**HAIDIMBA-VAIDAGDHYA**

**<MISSING_FIG href="../../../../../books_images/U-IMG-1690184516emage6.jpg"/>**

  The play herewith presented to the public, as Vidvan Manō Rañjani Series No. 20, is merely a compilation and does not lay any claim to originality. It may be stated in the outset that this piece entilled “Haidimba-Vaidagdhya” is merely an amplification of one of the most ancient plays in Samskṛit Literature. In fact, it is nothing but an adaptation of ‘Madhyama-Vyāyōga’, one of the smallest, and perhaps one of the most insignificant, of the numerous plays ascribed to Bhāsa, the Father of the Samskṛit Drama, who flourished in the 6th century B.C., during the earlier part of the reign of the great monarch ¹Śrī Harsha Vikramāditya

— — — — — — — —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  — —  —  —  —  —  
*¹Śrī Harsha Vikramāditya is mentioned by a host of Indian writers as one of the great Emperors of Ancient India at the time. He is said to have founded a new Śakābda called the Harsha Era (prevalent in Nēpāl and adjacent countries) in the year 457 B. C. — just 400 years before the commencement of the Mālava or the Vikrama Era — in commemoration of his having completely vanquished and driven out, in a great battle, the Śakas or the Persians who first began to make hostile incursions into India from the province of Sacae under their most powerful and illustrious monarch, Cyrus the Great in 550 B. C., (marking out the Śaka-Kāla which is reckoned) just 2526 years after the death of Yudhishṭhira, which is believed to have taken place in the beginning of the Laukika or Sapta*

of Ujjayinī surnamed Śakāri Vikramāditya, to distinguish him from the numerous other Vikramādityas, who have played an important part in the ancient history of India.

 There is a strong tradition to the effect that our poet belonged to a low class of Āryas, that he was the Washerman-in-Chief of king Śrī Harsha Vikramāditya at Ujjayinī, that *he wrote ²twenty-four dramas in all, and that his brother* rshi Era which commenced on Chaitra Sudi 1 of the year 26 of the Kali Yuga corresponding to 3076 B.C. (Vide Kalhaṇa's Rāja-Taraṅgiṇī; III. 124-129; Sōmadeva's Kathāsarit Sāgara Vishamaśila Lambaka; Bhartṛi Mēṇṭha's Hayagrīva-Vadha Nāṭakaः Prastāvanā; Rāmila's Maṇiprabhāः Prastāvanā ; Chitsukhāchārya's Bṛihat Śaṅkara Vijaya; Sadāśiva Brahmēndra's Guru-Ratna-Mālā; Atmabōdhēndra's Sushumā; my English Introduction to Ratnāvali and Nāgānanda entitled ‘Śriharsha the Dramatist'; my 'Age of Śaṅkara'ः Part I, Ch, II. pp. 24-25; and my “Kings of Magadha” ः pp-158-160)

²The following are the names of the twenty-four dramas said to have been composed by Bhāsa, and they are arranged here according to the source of the plot from which they have been taken.

 * A. Plays based on Vālmīki's Śrīmad Rāmāyaṇa ः*

1. Bāla-Charita (Bāla-Kāṇḍa), quoted by Viśvanātha Kavirāja's in his S. D. VI. 85.

2\. Pratimā-Naṭaka (Ayōdhyā and Araṇya Kāṇḍas) in 7 Acts.

3\. Abhishēka-Nāṭaka (Kishkindhā, Sundara and Yuddha Kāṇḍas) in 6 Acts.

poets and contemporaries, out of envy and prejudice, contem*p*tuously styled him Dhāvaka (Dhoby-Poet). One day, the

4\. Udātta-Rāghava (Uttarakāṇḍa), quoted by Rājaśēkhara in his Kavi-Vimarśa, by Śāradātanaya in his Bhāva-Prakāśa; by Dhanika in his 
D.R. II. 54, III. 3, III. 22, and by Viśvanātha Kavirāja in S. D. p. 129and 169.

  N. B. Out of these four plays, only Nos. 2 and 3 have been recently published in the Trivandrum Samskṛit Series.

*B. Plays based on Vyāsa's Mahābhārata ः*

5.Madhyama-Vyāyōga (Bhīmasēna saving a poor Brāhman family from the hands of Ghaṭōtkacha, his own son by Hiḍimbā, a Rākshasī, during the Pāṇḍava Vanavāsa) in 1 Act.

6. Pañcharātra (The story of Gōgrahaṇa during the Ajñāta Vanavāsa of the Pāṇḍavas and the marriage of Abhimanyu with Uttarā) in 3 Acts.

7. Dūta-Vākya (Śri Kṛishṇa as a Messenger of Peace before King Duryōdhana, just before the commencement of the Great War) in 1 Act.

8\. Dūta-Ghaṭotkacha (Ghaṭotkacha, on hearing the news of Abhimanyu's murder, goes out in great wrath and destroys the Kauravas in the disguise of a messenger of King Duryōdhana himself) in 1 Act.  


9\. Karṇa-Ehāra (The story of Karṇa, the great warrior, deprived of his *Kavacha* and *Kuṇḍala* by Indra, the King of the Gods, in disguise) in 1 Act.

10\. Uru-Bhaṅga (Bhīmasēna, splitting Duryōdhana's thigh in the Great War), a Tragedy in 1 Act.

* C. Plays based on the Purāṇas ः*

11\. Avimāraka (Story relating to the marriage of Vishṇusēna, son of Sauvīrarāja, with Kuraṅgi, daughter of Kunti Bhōja, a contemporary of the Pāṇḍavas) in 6 Acts.

12\. Bāla-Charita (The story of the birth and of the early history of Śrī Kṛishṇa) in 5 Acts.

13.Prabhāvatī (The story of the marriage of Prabhāvatī with Pradyumna, son of Śrī Kṛishṇa), quoted by Viśvanātha in S. D. p. 40 & 148.

  * N. B.* The first two plays of this series, viz, Nos. 11 & 12 have been already published in the Trivandram Samskṛit Series.

*D. Plays based on the Paiśāchī Bṛihatkathā of Guṇāḍhyaः*

14\. Udayana Charita (The story of Vatsarāja's marriage with Vāsavadattā), quoted by Dhanika in his D. R. II. 53; in Visvanātha's S.D. p. 169, and by Kālidāsa in his Mēghasandēśa.

15\. Pratijñã Yaugandharāyaṇa or Pratijñā-Nāṭaka (The story of Yaugandharāyaṇa effecting the escape of Vatsarāja with Vāsavadattā daughter of Pradyuba), in 4 Acts.

16\. Tāpasa-Vatsarāja, quoted by Abhinavagupta and Śiradātanaya in their works on Alaṅkāra.

hand, his attention so thoroughly engrossed with the bookthat he paid no heed to the clothes carried away by the wind, nor to the king approaching him. Śrī Harsha's curiosity was excited, and the washerman was taken to the

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

18\. Priyadarśikā, a Nāṭikā in 4 Acts.

19\. Rātnāvalī, a Nāṭikā in 4 Acts.

20\. Nāgānanda, a Nāṭaka in 5 Acts.

*N. B.* All the abovementioned dramas, excepting Tāpasa-Vatsarāja, have been published in the Trivandrum Samskṛit Series. The last three plays have been long ago published in various parts of India, under the name of Śrīharsha, who is generally mistaken for Harshavardhana Śīlāditya of Kanauj, the patron of Bāṇa.

*E. Semi-Historical Playsः*

21\. Chārudatta or Daridra-Chārudatta (of which Śūdraka's Mṛichchhakatika is merely an amplification) in 4 Acts.

22\. Kiraṇāvali (The story of the love of Mākanda and Kiraṇāvalī), a drama in 9 Acts abruptly said to end with the tragic catastrophe of the heroine, quoted by Rājaśēkhara, Śāradā-tanaya and by a host of Naiyāyikas, as a work which though containing benediction is still left unfinished.

* N. B.* The last play has not still seen the light of the day.

*F. Original Playsः*

23\. Mukuṭa-Tāḍitaka, quoted by Chaṇḍapāla in his Damayantī-Kāvya.

24\. Bhagavad-Ajjuka (A Farce in Prākṛit)



king together with his book, which turned out to be an exquisite drama — the Nāgānanda. The King was immensely pleased, and Bhāsa was at once raised to opulence and made a court-poet. It is said that he dedicated his last three plays — Priyadarśikā, Ratnāvalī and Nāgānanda — to his noble patron, Śrī Harsha, which thereafter went under the name of the king to whom they were ascribed by the poet himself.

Bhāsa (Bhāsaka) and Dhāvaka refer to one and the same individual, and these names are almost indiscriminately *i*nter-changed in the various editions, we have, of ³Māla-

\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_

*N. B.* These two plays also have not seen the light of the day. It is said that a copy of the last play has been found out by Rāmakṛisna Kavi, a great Telugu scholar, who has recently translated some of Bhāsa's plays into Telugu. It is suggested that Vāsavadattā, referred to by Bāṇa with great admiration, is not the paltry work of Subandhu, but a drama, composed by Saumila, one of Bhāsa's contemporary poets.

³For many generations past, Indian scholars have unanimously held that the three plays ascribed to Śrī Harsha were really written by a poet of Śrī Harsha Vikramāditya's court, called Bhāsa or Dhāvaka. The Kāvyaprakāśa of Mammaṭa Bhaṭṭa is the most important record of this view. Commenting on the stanza which reckons fame and wealth among the fruits bestowed by poetry — “ काव्यं यशसेऽर्थकृते ” etc. — the author saysः “ कालिदासादीनामिव दशः श्रीहर्षादेर्धावका (भासा) दीनामिव धनम् ” — Fame to such as Kālidāsa, and Wealth to such as Dhāvaka (Bhāsa) from Śrī Harsha etc. Prof. Cowell's statement is a very sorry one that these oral traditions are of but little historical value. Further, as has been already shown, the above  
vikāgnimitra and Kāvyaprakāśa, but the two names are *never found together in any work whatsoever.* Whether view has been adopted by all writers on Indian Rhetoric and Dramaturgy, ancient and modern ; and nothing but bare pre-judice can induce one to question the authority of such an old written record as the Kāvyaprakāśa. Thus Mahēśvara in his Prakāśādarśā, commenting on the above lines of Kāvyaprakāśa writes as follows ः – “श्रीहर्षो राजा । धावकेन (भासकेन) रत्नावल्यादीनि रूपकाणि तन्नाम्ना कृत्वा बहुधनं लब्धम्।” — that Dhāvaka (Bhāsaka) composed his Ratnāvalī and other plays in the name of his patron Śrī Harsha, and obtained much wealth from him. The same opinion is held by Nāgēsa Bhaṭṭa in his Udyōta, by Vaidyanātha in his Prakāśaprabhā, and by Jayarāma in his Prakāśatilaka, when commenting on the above lines of the Kāvyaprakāśa. Śrī Tārānātha Tarkavāchaspati, one of the greatest Samskṛit scholars of modern India, supports this view and writesः —

  “एतच्च, रत्नावल्यां वर्णितं सागरिकया सह वत्सराजसमागमवृत्तान्तमनुहरति । तस्य नाटकस्य धनप्रत्याशया कविना श्रीहर्षकृतया ख्यापितत्वेऽपिधावककृतत्वं, धावकरय च एतस्मात् कवेः (कालिदासात्) पूर्वकालिकत्वं, ग्रन्थकृतैव स्वयं प्रस्तावनाप्रसङ्गेऽभिहितमित्तत स्तद्वृत्तान्तमनुसृत्य, एतद्ग्रन्थकारेण निरमायीति प्रतिभाति ।”

— Vide his Preface to Mālavikāgnimitra, p. 8.

From this it is clear that this great Samskṛit Scholar holds Dhāvaka to be the author of Ratnāvalī, and to have lived earlier than Kālidāsa, who produced his drama called Mālavikāgnimitra in imitation of Bhasa's Ratnāvalī. In commenting on the passage in the Prastāvanā to Mālavikāgnimitra where reference is made by Kālidāsa to Dhāvaka (Bhāsaka, or Bhāsa) the name of the poet be Dhāvaka, Bhāsaka or Bhāsa, the circumstances noted by Kāvyaprakāśa are quite in keeping with the view which external and internal evidences alike go to uphold. For, the proper construction of the sentence from Mammaṭa's Kāvyaprakāśa is thisः — “(Among the *benefits conferred by poetry, come) fame such as ¹Kālidāsa* and Saumilla and other Kaviputras, Tārānātha saysः “धावकः
नागानन्दरत्नावल्यादिग्रन्थ- कारकः — Dhāyaka was the author of Nāgānanda, Ratnāvali aud other plays. Further Tārānātha Tarkavāchaspati has published his edition of Priyadarśikā under the name of Dhāvaka himself. It is thus clear from this great encyclopædic writer, whose “ Vāchasp̱atyam Bṛihadabhidhānam ” will ever stand as an imperishable monument of his Samskṛit erudition, that our poet, Bhāsa or Dhāvaka, is the real author of the three famous plays — Priyadarśikā, Ratnāvalī and Nāgānanda — though they now go under the name of Śrī Harsha.

* N. B.* It is a great pity that this fact has not attracted the attention of Mr. T. Gaṇapati Śāstriar, the learned editor of thirteen of Bhāsa's dramas in Trivandrum Samskṛit Series.

  ⁴ Among the Samskṛit poets there appear to be nine different persons who have written works at various times under the name of Kālidāsa. A good deal of misconception prevails amongst the modern scholars as to their respective lives and times on the one hand, and as to the number and nature of the works they have respectively written. Kālidāsa referred to here is Mātṛigupta or Kālidāsa I, who nourished in the court of Śrī Harsha Vikramāditya of Ujjain above referred to as the patron of Bhāsa. This poet was a contemporary of Pravarasēna II, the 5th king of the Third Gōnandiya Dynasty obtained from Śrī Harsha and wealth such as Dhāvaka *(Bhāsaka or Bhāsa) obtained from Śrī Harsha.” The* who reigned at Kāśmīr according to the correct interpretation of Kalhaṇa's Rāja-Taraṅgini for 60 years from 527 B. C. to 467 B. C. It is said that Mātṛigupta him self ruled Kāśmīr as Regent for Pravarasēna II under the orders of the great Emperor Śrī Harsha Vikramāditya for 4 years and 9 months from 532 B. C. to 527 B. C. He also appears to have been a contemporary of Ādi Śaṅkarāchārya who lived from 509 B. C. to 477 B. C., and it is this poet that introduced the Great Āchārya to Śrī Harsha Vikramāditya at Ujjayini, and Pravarasēna II at Kāśmīr. Rāmila, the author of Maṇiprabhā, and Mēnṭha, the author of Hayagrivavadha Nāṭaka, also appear to be his contemporaries. He is the famous author of the three dramas,

\(1\) Mālavikāgnimitra, written before he become a *portege* of Śrī Harsha Vikramāditya ;

\(2\) Vikramōrvaśīya, written in honour of his patron which secured him the kingdom of Kāśmīr; and

\(3\) Abhijñāna Śākuntala, referred by Kumārila Bhaṭṭa in his famous Ślōka-Vārtika on the Śābara Bhāshya, a commentary on the Pūrva Mimāmsā of Jaimini.

  In his dramas, Kālidāsa chiefly imitates Bhāsa, the author of the 24 dramas above referred to. Besides these three dramas, he is said to have composed a Prākṛit Poem called Rāvaṇa-Vadha under the name of Sētubandha Mahākāvya and in the name of his patron Pravarasēna II. King Rāmadāsa, the Ruler of Jayapur and a friend and contemporary of Akbar Jalāladdin, in commenting on this poem saysः

expression “ श्रीहर्षादेः ” standing as it does in the middle of the sentence, must be taken along with both extremes.

**“ धीराणां काव्यचर्चाचतुरिमविषये विक्रमादित्यवाचा**

**यं चक्रे कालिदासः कविकुमुदविधुः सेतुनामप्रबन्धम् ।**

**तद्व्याख्यासौष्टवार्थं परिषदि कुरुते रामदासः स एव**

**ग्रन्थं जल्लालदीन्द्रक्षितिपतिवचसा रामसेतुप्रतीपम् ॥ ”**

It consists of 15 Aśvāsakas, and the colophon at the end of each Āśvāsaka runs as followsः —

**“ इअ सिरिपवरसेणकारिए, कालिदासकए, दहमुहवहे, महाकाव्ये,**

**पन्दरहो आसासओ परिसमत्तो ”**

  This Kālidāsa must be carefully distinguished from the remaining eight Kālidāsas.

  (1) Kālidāsa II (Mēdhārudra) ः — He was a contemporary of Vikramārka, King of Mālava, who founded the Samvat or the Mālava Era of 57 B. C. He was one of the nine gems of his court, referred to in the famous stanza ः

**“ धन्वन्तरि-क्षपणका-Sमरसिंह-शङ्कु-**

**वेताळभट्ट-घटकर्पर-कालिदासाः।**

**ख्यातो वराहमिहिरो नृपतेः सभायां**

**रत्नानि वै वररुचिर्नव विक्रमस्य ॥ ”**

He is the author of the three Kāvyas, (I) Kumāra-Sambhava,. (II) Mēghadūta_and (III) Raghuvaṁśa. He is also said to have composed a treatise on Law and Ceremonials at the request of the king, and a work on Astronomy called Jyōtirvidā-bharaṇa. In the Śēshādhyāya of the latter work, the poet distinctly says ः

**“ काव्यत्रयं सुमतिकृद् रघुवंशपूर्वं**

**जातं ततो ननु कियच्छ्रुतिकर्मवादः। ”**

From this, — as has been already pointed out by me in my *English Introduction to Ratnāvalī published in 1902 under*

**“ ज्योतिर्विदाभरणकालविधानशास्त्रम् ।  
श्रीकालिदासकवितो हि ततो बभूव ॥ ”**

Jyōtirvidābharaṇa is evidently the last work of this Kālidāsa, and it was composed by him on the elapse of 3068 years after Kali, corresponding to 34 B. C., as he himself concludesः

**“ वर्षे सिन्धुर (८) दर्शना (६) Sम्बर (०) गुणैः (३) याते कलौ सम्मिते  
भासे माधवसञ्ज्ञितेऽत्र विहितो ग्रन्थकियोवक्रमः ॥ ”**  
Sushumā , a commentary by Ātmabōdhendra on Sadāśiva Brahmēndra's Gururatnamālā states that he was a contemporary of the 9th Āchārya of the Kāmakōṭi Pīṭha at Kāñchī surnamed Kṛipā Śankara who ruled the Maṭha from 33 B. C. to 8 A. D.

  (2) Kālidāsa III (Kōṭijit)ः — This Kālidāsa is said to be a contemporary and disciple of Mūka Śaṅkara, the 20th Āchārya who reigned at the Kāmakōṭi Piṭha for 39 years from 397 to 436 A. D. He is said to be the author of many erotic poems, such as (1) Ṛitu-Saṁhāra, (2) Śṛingāra-Tilaka, (3) Śruta-Bōdha (a work on Prosody); and he is also said to have written several Stōtras or Hymns of praise and adoration of Dēvi, such as Śyāmalā-Daṇḍaka, Navaratna-Mālā and a host of other minor poems in imitation of Saundarya-Laharī and Mūka-Pañchaśati, composed by his Spiritual Guru, Śrī Mūka Śaṅkara.

* N. B.* These three poets are known as “ Kālidāsa-Trayī ” or the Three Great Kālidāsas; and Rājaśekhara, the author of several renowned plays — who flourished in the beginning of the 5th century A. D according to our researches, but who is placed by European scholars between 884 A. D. and 959 A. D.

the title “ Śrīharsha the Dramatist ” — it follows that Śrī *Harsha Vikramāditya must have been the patron of both* — refers to these three Kālidāsas in his critical estimation of poets called Kavi-Vimarśa, as followsः —

**“ एकोऽपि जीयते हन्त ! कालिदासो न केनचित् ।  
शृङ्गारे लालितोद्गारे, कालिदासत्रयी किमु ॥ ”**

  (3) Parimala Kālidāsa or Kālidāsa IV (Padmagupta)ः — He is a great friend and contemporary of Bhōjadēva, King of Dhārā, who reigned according to all authentic accounts between 1014 and 1070 A. D. He is the author of a Mahākāvya known as Nava Sāhasāṅka Charita, celebrating the life of Sindhurāja or Sindhula, father of Bhōja. It is a beautiful historical poem composed in 18 cantos by Parimala Kālidāsa whose family name was Padmagupta. The colophon at the end of every canto describes him as the son of Mṛigāṅkadatta and he is no doubt the Kālidāsa who is said to have adorned the court of King Bhōja in the semi-historical work known as Bhōja-Prabandha. It is clear from this work that the father of Bhōja was also known as ‘ Vikramāditya ’ and ‘ Navasāhasāṅka ’ ; and that his capital was Dhārā, the ancient seat of rule of the Paramāra Kings of Mālava, and even now an important city in Central India. He might very well be the author of some of the best stanzas attributed to
Kālidāsas in Bhōja-Prabandha. He distinctly refers to the previous Kālidāsas and to their works, and he was chiefly instrumental in securing the throne to Bhōja from the hand of his wicked uncle Muñja. (Vide for full particulars to the English Preface of my Tamil Drama “ Bhōja Charitram ”, pp. VI-XII.)

  (4) Kālidāsa V (Yamakakavi)ः — He is the author of Nalōdaya or ‘ Rise of Nala ’ which describes the restoration to the poets referred to aboveः only Dhāvaka or Bhāsa must have lived in the earlier part of the reign of Sri Harsha

— — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — —

*power of King Nala after he had lost his all. The chief aim of the author is to show off his skill in the manipulation of the most varied and artificial metres, as well as all the elaborate tricks of style exhibited in the latest Kāvyas. Rhyme even is introduced, and that too, not only at the end of, but within metrical lines. It consists of 4 Uchchhvāsas and is evidently later than the Naīshadhīya Charita, a Mahākāvya by Śriharsha who belongs to the 12th century A. D.*

* (5) Nava Kālidāsa or Kālidāsa VIः — He is the author of the beautiful poem called Champū-Bhāgavata, composed in 6 Vilāsas; and the poet appears to be a non-Brāhman belonging to the Vēllāla caste in Southern India. At the end of the 6th Vilāsa, the poet describes himself as follows —*

***“ यस्मिन् शङ्करदारसौरभजरीजृम्भत्कटाक्षच्छटा-***

***पाताः कल्पलतावितानसुमनोगुम्भप्रियम्भावुकाः।***

***तस्याऽसौ नवकालिदासविदुषः षष्टोऽपि काव्यामृते***

***चम्पूनामनि धीमतां कृतसमुल्लासो विलासोऽगमत् ॥ ”***

*His commentator, Akkayasūri, in his Vyākhyā called Ratnāvalī, remarks about his parentage as followsः*

***“ बेल्लालकुलवाराशिजातशीतांशुनाऽमुना ।***

***कालिदासेन नव्येन शीतं श्रोत्रनभः कृतम् ॥***

***कालिदासस्य नव्यस्य निसर्गमधुरा गिरः।***

***टीका रत्नावळी भूषा तथाऽप्यासां भव त्वसौ ॥ ”***

*इह खलु वेल्लालकुलशिरोमणि स्तत्रभवान् नवकालिदासो नाम महाकविः सकलकल्याणगुणशरण्यस्य भगवतो वासुदेवस्य गुणक्रियास्वरूपप्रतिपादनाऽविनाभूततया*

Vikramāditya; and Kālidāsa I, the author of these famous plays — Mālavikāgnimitra, Vikramōrvaśīya and Śākuntala

— — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — —

*निरतिशयपुरुषार्थसाधनं..................लौकिकपुरुषार्थोपयिकं च भागवतचम्पूनामकं महाकाव्यं चिकीर्षुः..........आशीरूपं मङ्गलं..........निबध्नाति । ”*

* The poet, therefore, clearly appears to be a learned Samskṛit scholar belonging to the Vellāla caste and he probably fiourished in the 15th century A. D. He should not be however mistaken for the author of the Champū-Bhāgavata in 5 Taraṅgas, recently published in Telugu character under the name of Ambhōja or Varṇādhika Bhōja, a Telugu Niyōgi Brāhman generally known as Padmarāja.*

* (6) Akabarīya Kalidāsa or Kālidāsa VIIः — He was a contemporary of Akbar, the Great Mogal Emperor who flourished in the 16th century A. D. He is described in the Kāvya Mālā Series to have composed a number of Samasyās in Samskṛit language. (Vide Kāvya Mālā, Part I. p. 8, Foot-note — ‘ अकबरीयकालिदासस्तु चतुर्थोऽर्वाचीनः ’ )*

* (7) Kālidāsa VIII ः — He appears to be a native of the Tanjore District, and seems to be well-acquainted with the Brāhman widows with their shaven heads and the yellow-clothed Sannyāsins of Māyavaram on the banks of the Kāvēri. He appears to have lived about 200 years ago in the luxurious court of the Tanjore Palace. He is said to be the author of Prahasana Nāṭaka, a most obscene book that I have come across in the field of Samskṛit Literature. It is wrongly believed by some scholars, by the mere accidental coincidence of the name, to be the work of the same great poet, the author of the three famous dramas Śākuntala etc. It is a sacrilege to ascribe this worthless Prahasanam to such a highly noble and exalted poet as the first Kālidāsa or, indeed, to any other Kāli —*

must have lived during the latter part of the reign of the same famous Emperor, whom he has immortalised in unmistakable terms in his second drama, Vikramōrvaśīya.

— — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — —

*dāsas above described. The sooner the book is prescribed, the better.*

* (8) Abhinava Kālidāsa or Kālidāsa IX (Mādhava) ः — He is quite a modern writer who died recently about 8 years ago. He is said to have written or compiled the semihistorical work called Saṅkshēpa Śaṅkara Jaya, from the materials furnished to him in Ānandagiri's Prāchīna Śaṅkara Vijaya and Vyāsāchala Kavi's Śaṅkara Vijaya Kāvya. It is now ascribed by the publishers to Mādhavāchārya or the famous Vidyāraṇya Svāmin, who practically refounded the Śṛiṅgēri Maṭha in 1331 A. D., though it is not in his usual style of writing, and does not bear his name in the body of the book itself. As stated in the very first verse, — ‘ प्राचीनशङ्करजये सारः सङ्गृह्यतेमया ’ — it is merely a compilation chiefly from Prāchīna Śaṅkara Vijaya, and the description given of the author in the Introductory chapter is sufficient to indicate, that it is not the work of that great teacher Vidyāraṇya and that it was written only by a modern writer under the pseudonym of Mādhava (Nārāyaṇa) who calls himself as Nava Kālidāsa ः*

***“ सामोदैरनुमोदिता, मृगमदैरानन्दिता चन्दनैः,***

***मन्दारैरभिनन्दिता प्रियगिरा, काश्मीरजैः स्मेरिता ***

***वागेषा नवकालिदासविदुषो दोषोज्झिता दुष्कवि-***

***वातैर्निष्करुणैः क्रियेत विकृता धेनुस्तुरुष्कैरिव ॥ ”***

*(Vide for fuller particulars to note No. 138, pp. 145 to 157, of Part I, Chapter III of my “ Age of Śaṅkara ”),*

  The classical drama in the Saṁskṛit language practically begins with our poet, and Bhāsa might be well described as the Father of the Saṁskṛit Drama. Though we find the first traces of the Saṁskṛit Drama in some of the earliest hymns of the ⁵Ṛig Veda and the Zendavesta during so early

— — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — —  

^( *5*)*The origin of the Saṁskṛit Drama must be ultimately traced to some of the hymns in the Ṛig Vēda, which are in the form of Saṁvādas or dramatic dialogues between various personages described in them. As examples of these dramatic dialogues in the Vēdas, we may cite,*

*(I) Yamī-Yama Saṁvāda (Ṛig. X-10);*

*(II) Paṇi-Saramā Saṁvāda (Ṛig. X-108); and*

*(III) Urvaśī-Purūravas Saṁvāda (Ṛig. X-85).*

* The first is a Love-Poem, with Śṛiṅgāra (Erotic Sentiment) as its predominant Rasa, relating to the love between Yama and Yamī, the twin brother and sister, children of Vivasvat — the Adam and Eve of the Jews — and it appears to have been chosen by the Vēdic bard before the Brāhman Āryas settled in India. (Vide Adharva Vēda, XVIII. 3-13, which distinctly says that they are the first man and woman.) The second is a beautiful dialogue between Asura Paṇis and Dēva Saramā, with Vīra (Heroic Sentiment) as its predominant Rasa, and it illustrates the ancient Rāja-nīti of the Eastern Āryas before they became separated into Irānians and Indians. The third is a Love-plot in both aspects of Śṛiṅgāra — Sambhōga (Love in Union) and Vipralambha (Love in Separation) — and it depicts the love between King Purūravas and the nymph Urvaśī, which forms the foundation of Kālidāsa's famous drama, Vikramōrvaśīya, the plot of which had been already fully developed in the Śatapatha Brāhmaṇa and the Mahābhārata. (Vide for fuller*

an epoch as the Chhandas Period, at the latter half of Kṛita Yuga when the Eastern Āryas — the ancient Hindus and Īrānians were still living together under the various names of Dēvas and Asuras, north of the Himālayas in Central Asia; still it cannot be said that the drama had taken a definite shape during that period. There is no doubt that we find the names of many of the ancient Saṁskṛit dramas⁶, as

— — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — —

*partīculars to my “ Daśarūpakōtpatti ” in Saṁskṛit, published 1 as an appendix to my stage edition of Nāgānanda in 1903).*

*⁶As examples of some of these ancient Saṁskṛit dramas we may mention the followingः*

*(1) Samudra-Mathana — It is a Samavakāra said to have been composed by Pitāmaha or Brahmadēva; and according to Bharata Muni, it is said to have been enacted for the first time before the Dēvas during the festival called Indradhvaja Mahōtsava.*

*(2) Tripura-Dāha — It is a Dima, also ascribed to Pitāmaha, and is said to have been first enacted before the Great God Paramēśvara, on the northern slope of the Himālayas by Bharata Muni himself with the Celestial nymphs (Apsarases) as actors and actresses.*

*(3) Lakshmī-Svayaṁvara — It is a Nāṭaka said to have been composed by Sarasvati, the consort of Pitāmaha, and it is said to have been enacted before Indra's court by Apsarases with the help and training of the Sage Bharata. Kālidāsa also alludes to the performance of this drama in his Vikramōrvaśiya, where Urvaśi and Mēnakā are said to have taken the part (Bhūmikā) of Lakshmī and Vāruṅī respectively.*

*(4) Jāmadagnya-Jaya — It is a Vyāyōga, like the present. play, said to have been written by Bharata Muni himself. It*



distinct dramatic pieces, mentioned in the works of ancient authors such as Bharata's Nāṭya-Śāstra and Patañjali's.

— — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — —  
*alludes to the defeat of Kārtavīryārjuna or to the subjugation of the military tribe by the Brāhmanical hero Paraśu Rāma, the son of Jamadagni. The Daśarūpaka also specifies this as an example of the Vyāyōga.*

*(5) Kusumaśc̄khara-Vijaya — It is a Rūpaka of the class of Îhāmṛiga, one of the ten classes of the Saṁskṛit major dramas, also ascribed to Bharata Muni.*

*(6) Śarmishṭhā-Yayāti — It is an Aṅka (one of the ten kinds of Rūpakas or major plays) also ascribed to Bharata Muni, and it is said to have been enacted for the first time before the great king Nahusha, while he was holding the high office of Mahēndra, the king of the Dēvas.*

*(7) Saugandhikā-haraṇa-It is a drama in one act, relating to the rape of a princess named Saugandhikā which directly contravenes, as is the case in the present drama, the rule of modern rhetoricians relating to the exclusion of love and females in the play, In the Buddhistic accounts of Maudgalyāyana and Upatishya, two of the direct disciples of Gautama Buddha, it appears that this play was enacted before them on a certain auspicious day at Rājagṛiha, the capital of the Magadha Kings at the time.*

*(8) Kaṁsa-Vadha or ‘ the Slaying of Kaṁsa ’ and*

*(9) Bali-Bandha or ‘ the Binding of Bali ’.*

*These two plays belong to the class of Nāṭakas and are ascribed to the great Grammarian Pāṇini and are distinctly referred to in his great poem called “ Pātāla Vijaya ”. We learn from the Mahābhāshya, that these two plays were enacted by Saubhikas in Patañjali's time.*



Mahābhāshya, in which latter work, we find a few verses and passages quoted from some of these dramas. But none of them have come down to our time.

The poet has taken up for his subject a fine episode from the Mahābhārata, just as he took up before the story of Rāmāyaṇa as the subject of his excellent dramas, Bāla-Charita, Pratimā Nāṭaka, Abhishēka Nāṭaka and Udātta-Rāghava. This is first of the plays which he had evolved from the Mahābhārata, — Pāncharātra, Dūta-Vākya, Dūta Ghaṭōtkacha, Kaṛna-Bhāra, and Ûru-Bhaṅga, being his other plays based upon the story of Mahābhārata. The two Great Indian Epics, the Rāmāyaṇa of Vālmīki, and the Mahābhārata of Kṛishṅa Dvaipāyana Vyāsa, form aninexhaustible treasure of religious and moral instruction, imparted not by dry precepts but enforced by the history of living men, princes and warriors, sages and hermits, in fact persons of every specimen of humanity that can interest man at all times and in all countries. The Mahābhārata in particular is the Hindu's perfect storehouse of Religion and Philosophy, Profit and Pleasure. It is his Dharma- Śāstra and Artha-Śāstra; it is his history and biography; it is his astronomy; it is his arithmetic; it is the means of his self-knowledge; it is the means of his acquisition of emancipation. As the well-known saying goes
— “ भारतः पञ्चमः वेदः ” — it is regarded as the supplement of the Vēdas in the matter of understanding Brahman. In brief — “ न तदस्ति हि लोकेऽस्मिन् भारते यन्न दृश्यते ” — the Mahābhārata is the one book that is held to be of great value in India. Western scholars used to compare the Mahābhārata to a national bank of unlimited resources upon which succeeding poets and dramatists, age after age, have freely drawn the plots of their respective poems and dramas, without being able to bring about a sensible diminution ; and consequently a knowledge of Mahābhārata enables one to understand without any diffi-culty the plots of nearly all the dramas and poems written either in Saṁskṛit or in the Indian vernaculars in later times. It has been truly remarked by Sir Rabīndra Nāth Tagore, one of the greatest of our modern poetsः “ Hence it is that centuries have rolled on, but the Rāmāyan and the Mahābhārat have flowed through India with undiminished volume. They are read daily in every village, in every house, — as welcome in the grocer's shop as in the royal palace. Blessed are the two poets whose true names have been lost in the vast wilderness of Time, but whose words still flow, carrying a copious stream of strength and peace to the doors of millions of men and women, and fertilising the heart of modern India with rich loam incessantly brought down from hundreds of past centuries. Therefore, it will not be correct to call the Rāmāyan and the Mahābhārat epics only; they are histories to ; — not the history of incidents, which concerns a particular age only, but the ‘ Eternal History of India ’. Other histories change with the passage of time; but this history has suffered no change. The history of what has been the object of India's devoted endeavour, of India's adoration, and of India's resolve, is seated on the throne of Eternity in the Palace of these two vast Epics.”

  It has been well said by Sir Monier Williams, one of the greatest of Saṁskṛit scholars in Europeः “ No one too can read either the Rāmāyaṇa or the Mahābhārata, without feeling that they rise above the Homeric poems, in this — that a deep religious meaning appears to underlie the narrative and that the wildest allegory may be intended to conceal a sublime moral, symbolising the conflict between good and evil, and teaching the hopelessness of victory in so terrible a contest without purity of soul, self-abnegation and subjugation of passions.” This small drama also is inspired by the same high ideals and is accordingly a genuine and true representation of our ideal life and deserves our warmest regard and love.

  The plot of the original play called Madhyama Vyāyōga had been, no doubt, taken by Bhāsa from the story of Hidimbā and Ghaṭōtkacha as depicted in the Ādi Parvan of the Mahābhārata; and the story of Bhīmasēna saving a poor Brāhman family from the hands of Ghaṭōtkacha is clearly a counter-part of the story of Bakāsura and his subjugation by Bhīmasēna while living at a Brāhman's house in Ekachakrāpura as depicted in the Adhyāyas relating to Baka-Vadha Parvan. The story of the original play has been briefly narrated by Mahāmahōpādhyāya Pandit M. Lakshmaṇa Sūri in his valuable Saṁskṛit Introduction to this amplification and adaptation of the said play. The narrative as depicted by the poet is, indeed, very simple; and I do not know whether the poet intended it, or not, but there is a running under-current of suggestion in the original play itself, which shows that the poet had a certain definite purpose in dramatising this simple plot. In adapting the play to the modern requirements of the present stage, I have tried my best to bring out, in clear and unambiguous terms, the said main object of the play, and the many valuable lessons intended to be illustrated by it by enforcing high moral and spiritual truths, and by presenting, as it were in a nut-shell, the quint-essence of all our ancient religion and philosophy by enlarging the drama with appropriate quotations and songs, chiefly taken from Bhāsa's own other works, supplemented from the works of great poets like Kālidāsa, Bhavabhūti, Jayadēva etc.

  The following is a brief account of the marriage of Hiḍimbā with Bhīmasēna, and of the birth of Ghaṭōtkacha, son of Bhīmasēna by Hidimbā, as narrated in the Mahābhārata. We are told that after Pāṇḍu's death, when the five Pāṇḍava brothers were first taken to Hastināpura with their revered mother Kuntī, (Yudhishṭhira was 16 years of age, Bhīmasēna 15 years, Arjuna 14 years, and the twins Nakula and Sahadēva 13 years). They managed to live for 13 years with Dhṛitarāshṭra and his sons. Duryōdhana, being jealous of the enormous power wielded by the Pāṇḍavas, and of the exalted position to which Yudhishṭhira had been raised was terribly anxious to get rid of them; and in consultation with his wicked maternal uncle Śakuni, he induced the five Pāṇḍava brothers to go to Vāraṇāvata and stay there in a house specially constructed by him out of inflamable materials. His object was, that after the Pāṇḍavas had stayed there for some time, to set fire to the said house, and to have the Pāṇḍavas with their mother burnt to death with the said house. But fortunately they were apprised in time, of his foul plans, by Vidura. The Pāṇḍavas were, at first, taken by surprise; but as an attempt to run away from the place was not advisable, they prepared an under-ground passage and set fire to the lac-house themselves and made their escape disguised as Brāhmans.

  At that time, Bhīmasēna was 28 years old and the brothers still remained unmarried. They first arrived at a place which was inhabited by a Rākshasa named Hiḍimba, ‘a cannibal of frightful appearance, with red eyes, long teeth, and a complexion as black as the rain cloud.’ Seeing that some persons had entered within his limits, he sent his sister Hiḍimbā to inquire who they were and the purpose which brought them. She set out on her errand, but on arriving at the place, was so charmed with the handsome appearance of Bhīma that far from carrying out that behest of his brother, she stood gazing at him completely enamoured and was still standing there; when her brother, seeing that she had not returned as soon as she ought, himself came over to the place, eager to inquire about the real state of things. He now realized what had happened and reproaching his sister for her fickle nature he rushed towards her. Bhīma interceded on her behalf, and he was, of course, quite a match for him, and a violent struggle ensued. At last Bhīma succeeded in catching hold of the giant's waist; and lifting him up, threw him down on the ground with such violent force, that his bones were broken to pieces and he died. Left without a protector, Hiḍimbā offered to marry the victor; and on the express condition⁷ that she should live separately from him as soon as a son was born to her and that she should come to him whenever he desired her in privacy. Bhīmasēna married her with the consent of his aged mother Kuntī and his elder brother Yudhishṭhira; and from that time he lived with her continuously for six months. At the end of this period, she conceived, and a powerful son, Rākshasa in appearance and power, was  
— — — — —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —

***“ समक्षं भ्रातृमध्ये तु तां चोवाच स राक्षसीम् ।  
शृणु राक्षसि सत्येन समयं ते वदाम्यहम् ॥ १५ ॥  
यावत्कालेन भवति पुत्रस्योत्पादनं शुभे ।  
तावत्कालं चरिष्यामि त्वया सह सुमध्यमे ॥ १६ ॥

उत्तमस्त्रीगुणोपेता भजेथा वरवर्णिनि ॥ १७ ॥ ”***

  भीम उवाच,*

***“ सहवासो मया जीर्णस्त्वया कमलपालिके ।  
पुनर्द्रक्ष्यसि राज्यस्था नित्यभाषत तां तदा ॥ २१ ॥ ”***

* हिडिम्बोवाच,*

***“ यदा मां संस्मरेः कान्त ! रिरंसू रहसि प्रभो ।  
तदा तव वशं भूय आगन्तास्म्याऽऽशु भारत ॥ २२ ॥  
इत्युक्त्वा सा जगामाशु भाव मासज्य पाण्डवे ।  
हिडिम्बा समयं स्मृत्वा स्वां गतिं प्रत्यपद्यत ॥ २३ ॥ ”***

immediately born to them and he was called⁸ Ghaṭōtkacha, because he had a head like an earth pot (Ghaṭa) and a tuft with erect hairs (Utkacha) and he was to be a great help to the Pāṇḍavas in the ensuing Great War which was fought at Kurukshētra.

  The time of the play is laid during the interval of the period of exile of the Pānḍavas in the forest for 12 years ;

— — — —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —  —

***“ ततोऽलभत सा गर्भं राक्षसी कामरूपिणी ।

प्रणम्य विकचः पादावगृह्णात् स पितुस्तदा ॥ १२ ॥  
मातुश्च परमेष्वास स्तौचनामास्य चक्रतुः।  
घटोहास्योत्कच इति माता तं प्रत्यभाषत ॥ १३ ॥  
अभवत् तेन नामाऽस्य घटोत्कच इति स्मह ।  
अनुरक्तश्चता नासीत् पाण्डवान् स घटोत्कचः ॥ १४ ॥ ”***



and nearly 30 years had, at least, elapsed since Ghaṭōtkacha was born to Hiḍimbā by Bhīmasēna who was himself then about 58 years old. For according to the Mahābhārata (I.134) the Pāṇḍavas after leaving Hiḍimbā’s forest lived for 6 months in Ekachakrāpura disguised as Brāhmans in a Brāhman's house; when it was that Baka, a friend of Hiḍimba and an eater of Brāhmans was slain by Bhīmasēna at the request of his revered mother Kuntī to save the family of the Brāhman in whose house they were living. They then continued their journey through the Matsyas, the Trigartas, the Kīchakas and Pāñchālas and arrived at the Capital of King Drupada, who was holding a great Sva-Yaṁvara for the bestowal of the hand of his daughter Draupadī. Kings and princes from all parts of the country had gathered there, and the Pāṇḍavas too went to the place disguised as Brāhmaṇas, where they lived for one full year. After their marriage with Draupadī, the position of the Pāṇḍavas had become considerably strengthened and they successfully managed to go back to Hastināpura, where they again lived with Dhṛitarāshṭra and his sons for a period of 5 years at the end of which period the Pāṇḍavas and Kauravas effected a division of their kingdom, and Yudhishṭhira was crowned King at Indraprastha, then an altogether uncultivated tract of land, full of forests, which the Pāṇḍavas turned into a most beautiful city with the help of Viśvakarman, where Yudhishthira continued to reign for an un-interrupted period of 23 years. The pomp and eclat with which the Rajasūya was celebrated by Yudhishṭhira with the help of his brothers, who had nearly conquered the whole of India, had excited the jealousy of Duryōdhana once again, who, in consultation with Śakuni and Karṇa, brought about the game of dice, wherein Yudhishṭhira staked everything that he possessed on the surface of the earth, and which resulted in the Pāṇḍavas going in exile for 12 years, passing the thirteenth “ *incognito* ”.

The story of the present play as amplified by me under the name of Haiḍimba Vaidagdhya or as more fully expressed “ Haiḍimba - Praṇaya-Vaidagdhya ” — “ Haiḍimbā's Love-Stratagem ” — may be briefly summarised as followsः —

**INTRODUCTORY**

Agreeably to the conditions laid on them by Dhṛitarāshṭra, the five Pāṇḍava brothers had left Hastināpura and were now spending their period of exile with their faithful wife Draupadī in the Hermitage of Śālihōtra near the forest of Hiḍimbā Devi, on the southern slope of the Himālayas, where the latter had been living a life of Niyama with her son Ghaṭotkacha and his Rākshasa followers. Excepting Bhīmasēna, the middlemost (Madhyama) amongst the sons of Kuntī, all the other brothers including Draupadī who had been specially invited for a great sacrifice which was celebrated by Dhaumya, their family priest, had gone away to the Hermitage of Dhaumya, leaving Bhīmasēna alone behind them to look after their hermitage.

Hiḍimbā, his most dutiful wife, who had been, ever since her separation from her dear lord, leading a life of penance in that sacred forest, hitherto inhabited only by the Rākshasas, casually comes to learn from her Rākshasī friends that Bhīmasēna was living alone near the precincts of that forest and that her union with him 30 years ago in the same forest was still dearly remembered by her lord. Desirous of securing the pleasure of her lord's company once again, after that period of long separation, during which they had lived apart from each other, even without knowing each other's welfare, Hiḍimbā devises in her own Rākshasī-like fashion, a clever stratagem by which Bhīmasēna might be induced to go over to her place without her movements being observed even by her dearest companions. Knowing fully well that there was no other human being within the entire precints of that forest excepting Bhīmasēna, — now his brothers and wife having gone away from the hermitage, she pretends with her simple-hearted and brave son Ghaṭōtkacha, that she had taken a vow and that will be completed only on her securing the flesh and blood of a human being answering to the description of a Mahāpurusha as her diet on the close of the Upavāsa-pāraṇā, and that he should go about and seek a man answering to the said description within the precints of that sacred forest, and bring him alive within that evening to be disposed of by her as she liked. She also inwardly wishes that his giant-like son Ghaṭōtkacha should be humbled by her lord, as he was very proud of boasting that there was no one equal to him in the whole world in duel knowing full well that her lord was more than a match for him in that art of fighting.

SCENE I — Placeः A mountainous forest near the Hermitage of the Pāṇḍavas in Kurujāngalā. Time ः Afternoon.

The artless Ghaṭōtkacha, without knowing the real motive of his mother, comes out with his ferocious appearance, in search of a Mahāpurusha to be taken as food by his revered mother for her evening Upavāsa-pāranā, to the very same hermitage in which Bhīmasēna was living. Though born as a Rākshasa, he knows and feels that this act was wrong and sinful; but his great respect for his mother makes him obey her command at any cost. Just then, by accidence, a poor Brāhman family, consisting of an aged Brāhmaṇa, (Kēśavadāsa), his wife Suśīlā (Brāhmaṇa), and his three major sons, Kuśika (Jyēshṭha), Sugata (Madhyama), and Bharata (Kanishṭhā), happen to pass through the said forest on their way from Upagrāma, an · Agrahāra in Kurujāngala to the house of one Yajñabandhu, the maternal uncle of the old man, who was living in a village called Udyamagrāma in the northern portion of the said forest, to attend the Upanayana ceremony of his son, which was being celebrated there. Ghaṭōtkacha after wandering for a long time in the forest in search of a person answering to the description given to him by his mother, notices them with joy, and finds to his satisfaction that one of the sons of this old Brāhman bore all the characteristics of a Mahāpurusha, just neither too young, nor too old; and with a view to carry away one of them, he threatens them with his fearful appearance, and the poor Brāhman family becomes terribly afraid of him and entreats him by appealing to his mercy in various ways to let them go unhurt. Ghaṭōtkacha allows them to go uninjured, on one condition only, viz, that one of them should go with him to be eaten away by his mother; and finding that there was no other alternative left to them to effect any escape, each member of this noble Brāhman family readily offers himself or herself as a prey to be devoured by the Rākshasī after her fast, to save the rest of the members from destruction. The Brāhmaṇi's offer is sternly refused by Ghaṭōtkacha on the ground that his mother did not want a *woman* for her prey, and Kēśavadāsa is rejected on the ground that he was *too old*. The father is unwilling to part with his *eldest son*; and the mother is equally not inclined to sacrifice her *youngest son*. Sugata, the *middlemost* amongst the sons of the Brāhmaṇa, gladly and willingly offers himself to be sacrificed, and persuades the rest to part with him to be taken away by theRākshasa as a prey to his fasting mother.

Ghaṭōtkacha finds in Madhyama all the great qualities of a Mahāpurusha and thinks that he is just the sort of person required by her mother; and he gladly accepts his offer. Sugata takes his final leave of his aged and revered parents and his beloved and affectionate brothers who can scarcely tear themselves from his dear and sweet company. Led by Ghaṭōtkacha, Madhyama leaves the place with the greatest satisfaction in his heart that a golden opportunity had presented itself to him to do Parōpakāra-good to others-in his own humble way, which he considers to be his highest duty. He makes only one request of the Rākshasa that he may be allowed a few minutes to go to an adjoining stream to clense himself and perform his evening prayer — *Sāyam Sandhyā* and make himself ready for his great journey into the other world; and Ghaṭōtkacha, who had the greatest admiration for his undaunted courage and unparallelled self-sacrificing spirit, readily grants his prayer and goes away with him.

The unfortunate parents bereft of their dear and promising son, the crown - iewel of their family, become bewildered and even mad for a time; and immersed in deep sorrow, they lose their consciousness and fall down senseless on the ground. The young boy cannot bear this miserable sight and swoons supported by the eldest son, who after pathetically describing the situation, and reflecting on the miserable plight to which his mother country had been reduced and the utter helplessness of man in counter-acting against the evil effects of fate, also faints and falls down.

**Scene 2** — Place ः On the bank of a mountain stream in the same forest. Timeः Sun-set.

The Brāhman's son, Madhyama, enters, soliloquising upon the evanescent nature of human life and existence, with his mind perfectly tranquil and calm, and with his heart full of joy and contentment. He rejoices at the thought of his having been able to save his family from utter destruction by offering his own worthless body as food to be eaten away by the Rākshasa's mother after her fast. The serene and peaceful scenery around him, with the glorious sun setting on the bank of the river near by, with its tall and shadowy trees and bowers, inspires him with the true spirit of his Sanātana Religion and makes him all the more devoted to the Supreme Godः and he goes out to the river to bathe in its sacred waters and perform his evening Sandhyāvandana by offering his grateful prayers to the Lord of all Creation, the Great Nārāyaṇa.

As Madhyama is leaving the place, we see his aged father Kēśavadāsa entering the stage in great sorrow, with tears overflowing his eyes, searching after his most belove and noble-hearted son. Finding him no where, he takes him to be dead and to have been already eaten away by the Rākshasī ; and he cries out most pathetically over the cruel fate which had overtaken his son. Without this son, the whole world appears to him empty, and in his madness he raves after him. He blames himself for having foolishly consented to part with his son, instead of quitting his life then and there; and at the thought of the sad fate of his. young daughter-in-law, he resolves to put an end to his life. He knows that Ātma-Hatti (self-slaughter) is the most grievous of all sins which lead man to the lowest depths of Hell; and yet he cannot but think that this is after all the best course left open to him in this miserable world. He becomes choked with grief, feels himself completely exhausted and falls down senseless on the ground, mourning over the sad fate of his dear son and of his beloved wife.

Ghaṭōtkacha, who had been waiting for a long time for the return of the Brāhman’s son from the river, grows impatient at his non-arrival in time, and fears that it will be unfortunately too late for his revered mother to have the promised food after her fast that evening. Accordingly he comes in search of the young Brāhmaṇa and mistaking Kēśavadāsa who had been lying there without consciousness, for his son Madhyama, he revives him and raises him up; but to his great disappointment he finds him to be the aged father of that unfortunate boy. In his impatience he forgets even the usual civility, and asks the old father to call out his son and send him at once with him. Kēśavadāsa takes it as a fresh insult hurled against him, and vexedly replies that it was too much even for a Rākshasa to tease him in that way. Ghaṭōtkacha keenly feels for his rude behaviour towards the Brāhman and fréely apologises to him. But as it was getting too late for his mother to take her promised food after the fast, which was to be completed by that evening, Ghaṭōtkacha requests the old Brāhman to let him know at least the name of his son. The poor father cries out “ Ah ! Madhyama ! ” and swoons once again. Ghaṭōtkacha now takes “ Madhyama ” to be the name of the Brāhman's son and calls him aloud.

Bhīmasēna, the middle brother among the sons of Kuntī, who had been taking his usual evening exercise (Vyāyāma) near that place, hearing a loud voice calling out “ Madhyama ! Madhyama !! ” thinks that some body requires him and comes up and asks Ghaṭōtkacha why he was so loudly and frequently calling out for him. At first sight they become attracted at each other's appearance, and Bhīmasēna, though he had not seen such a person before, feels a strong and unaccountable affection for the Rākshasa who is equally affected by a powerful and irresistable feeling of reverence and respect towards that stranger.

The father and son had not personally known each other before; for Bhīmasēna had gone away from the company of Hiḍimbā, as soon as she brought forth Ghaṭōtkacha and for the last thirty years or more they had not met each other. Though Ghaṭōtkacha had frequeutly heard of his father and of his great valour, he could not recognise Bhīmasēna; and he says that he did not want him, but he was calling out only for Madhyama. Bhīmasēna replies that he is the *Madhyama* in every sense of the term, and Ghaṭōtkacha is surprised to learn that he should also be called Madhyama.

This conversation is over-heard by Kēśavadāsa who gradually gains his consciousness, in the confidence that this personage must be Bhīmasēna himself. Just then Madhyama, the Brāhman's son, turns up singing the glory of the Lord, presents himself before the Rākshasa and says that he is now ready to go with him to his mother to be devoured by her after her fast. Kēśavadāsa hastens to Bhīmasēna and entreats him to save his innocent son and his poor family from the hands of the wicked giant. Bhīmasēna readily offers his protection against the oppressor of the Brāhmans; and from the mutual conversation that follows he finds out that the Rākshasa from whom this unfortunate Brāhman family has to be protected by him, was no other than his own son by his eldest wife Hiḍimbā, although the rest do not know anything about this relationship. In gentle terms he rebukes the young giant and persuades him to change his mind and to abstain himself from doing this most sinful and cruel act; but Ghaṭōtkacha curtly replies that he could not change his mind, as he must obey his mother's command, however wicked and unrighteous it may be.

Bhīmasēna willingly offers himself in place of the Brāhman’s son, but the latter could not consent to any such proposal. He says that Bhīmasēna’s life is most valuable and that he should live for hundreds of years to protect his subjects. Bhīmasēna in vain tries to persuade him that it is the Kshattriya’s chief duty to protect all his subjects from their oppressors even at the sacrifice of their own lives, and that he could not see an innocent Brāhman in his own country suffer such oppression; but the young Brāhman would not listen to this. Ghaṭōtkacha thinks that Bhīmasēna would prove a better substitute and wants to take him away by force as a more desirable food for his mother’s fast. Bhimasēna inwardly admires, his son’s valour and a humorous conversation ensues between them. Ghaṭōtkacha boasts of the power of his arms, and Bhimasēna says that if he be so powerful, he must carry him by force. Ghaṭōtkacha laughs at this man’s impudence and says that it is a very easy matter with the son of Hiḍimbā and the grandson of Pavana and asks Bhīmasēna to be afraid of his extra-ordinary power. Bhīmasēna quickly replies that he does not know what fear is ! and that he will be glad to learn the same from his young son. Ghaṭōtkacha takes it as an insult, and asks him to know that he is the powerful son of Vṛikōdara; and in great anger asks him to take up his arms. his arms. Bhīma says that his arms are his only weapons and that he can destroy the whole world with the strength of his arms without any other assistance. “ This is true only of my father ” says the young giant, and taking this as an additional insult, he rushes against Bhīmasēna and drags him with mighty force; but he finds that he is quite unable to move him even an inch. Bhīmasēna gently laughs and Ghaṭōtkacha grows excited. The young Rākshasa pulls out a big tree from the forest and flings it against the powerful stranger, but lo ! the mighty warrior is not affected even a little by this terrible blow, and only the tree is broken into several pieces and falls down. He then plucks out a mighty slab from the top of a big mountain and hurls it with all his force against the chest of the stranger, but alas! it is ground to dust ! and Bhīmasēna simply laughs over the young giant's boasted valour. Ghaṭōtkacha then challenges Bhīmasēna to fight with him in a duel; and even in that single combat he is signally defeated. He now crys out to his father for help, without knowing that Bhīmasēna himself was his respected father. Then a device strikes him and by calling forth a spring of water and uttering a *Mantra*, with his hands and mouth purified in that water, he binds Bhīmasēna with a *Māyā-pāśa* which had been given to him by his mother. Bhīmasēna seems for a time quite vanquished, to the great disappointment and mortification of his Brāhman spectators; but in a minute, he remembers the *Māyā-pāśa-mōkshaṇa* *Mantra* which had been taught to him by the Great Lord Mahēśvara Himself, and he wants some body to help him with a little water to wash his hands and mouth, to enable him to utter the Mantra, and free himself from the effect of Māyā-pāśa. The young Brāhman rushes out at once to the river to bring him some water, and in the meantime, the old Brāhman pours out some water into the hands of
Bhīmasēna from his own Kamaṇḍalu and Bhīmasēna frees himself from the bonds of the Pāśa.

Ghaṭōtkacha feels quite subdued by the man, and iscompletely humiliated. Fearing that his mother would feel greatly disappointed by his not having brought a proper for her food after the fast, he now humbly requests Bhīmasēna to remember his first promise that he would follow him in place of the young Brāhman; and the latter then readily consents to go with him, and asks Ghaṭōtkacha to lead the way. Just as he starts, his right eye throbs, and he vaguely suspects some agreeable dodge in the matter. As they proceed, the old Brahman calls out his son, and they are eager to follow Bhīmasēna to see the turn of events, after they had first gone and comforted the Brāhmaṇī and her two sons by announcing to them this joyful news.

**Scene 3** — Placeः A pleasure garden in front of Hiḍimbā's Palace in the said forest. Timeः Early part of the night.

Hiḍimbā , the revered Queen of the Rākshasas is discovered in her most beautiful appearance, seated on a finemarble slab in the middle of a charming bower of jasmine,creepers in her pleasure garden, attended by two of her most confidential companions, Mandayānā and Karēṇukā. She islove-sick and has dressed herself in a simple and elegant style, befitting her double character as *Vāsakasajjikā* and *Virahōtkanṭhitā*, and is eagerly expecting the arrival of her dear lord Bhīmasēna. Ghaṭōtkacha, whom she had sent to fetch Bhīmasēna under the pretext of bringing a Mahāpurusha to complete her evening fast, does not arrive in time, and she feels anxious over the matter. The moon-lit midsummer night has already commenced with all its grand paraphernalia, augmenting Hiḍimbā’s pain ofseparation by the absence of her lover and she begins to doubt that Bhīmasēna might not at all be living in the said forest, and thinks that her companions must have given her false hopes, simply with a view to console her in her long separation from her dear lord. Mandayānā confirms her statement by assuring her that she had seen her lord longing after her sweet company with her own eyes and had even personally spoken to him.

Hiḍimbā, then for the first time , reveals to her companions, the secret plan by which she had devised to bring her lord there; and the companions applaud her for her clever love-stratagem. They assure her that their most beloved and powerful master, the valiant Ghaṭōtkacha, would surely bring her dear lord to her palace. A humorous conversation ensues between the mistress and and her maids who vie with each other in making Hiḍimbā's already beautiful appearance and apparel as perfect as possible. It was getting late, and still Ghaṭōtkacha had not turned up. Numerous thoughts begin to over-crowd and torment her love-sick brain and she feels terribly hopeless in her attempts. Her pain of separation from her dear lord now increases a thousandfold, and her companions have very hard task in trying to console her and revive her drooping spirit.

Just then Ghaṭōtkacha and Bhīmasēna approach the front gate of the garden, and the former takes leave of the latter saying that he will go and announce his arrival to his mother, and requests him to take his seat in the mean time in the outer pial there.

On hearing their voice, Hiḍimbā and her companions eagerly look up for Ghaṭōtkacha, who approaches his mother and pays his respectful salutations to her. She eagerly embraces her son and lovingly enquires of him if he had brought the person she wanted. At this query, Ghaṭōtkacha becomes greatly disconcerted and humiliated; and with a painful heart he replies in a submissive tone that a Mahāpurusha has come there of his own accord, although he himself had been quite powerless to bring him by force. Hiḍimbā’s hopes revive, and she eagerly asks him what sort. of a man he is. From the description given of the person by her son, Hiḍimbā takes it for certain that he must be her own dear and mighty lord Bhīmasēna, and goes out with her son to meet him.

Bhīmasēna, in the meantime, meets with good omens forecasting his sexual union with a most beautiful lady in the near future, and he vaguely guesses that all this must be a skilful stratagem played by his own dear Love, Kamalapālikā[^1] to bring him to her palace. He himself is eager to see Hiḍimbā, and intently longs for her sweet and pleasant company.

[^1]: # "Kamalapālikā is an other name of Hiḍimbā, and though born a Rākshasī, she is considered to be the Goddess Sālaka-"

As she proceeds to the gate, Hiḍimbā looks up at the stranger from a distance, admires at his exquisite beauty and perfection and is lost in astonishment. Ghaṭōtkacha, on the other hand, as he sees Bhīmasēna once again, finds all his wonted haughtiness suddenly disappearing; and a strong feeling of reverence and affection overtaking his proud heart.

In the meanwhile, Kēśavadāsa, with all the members of his family comes up to the spot in search of Bhīmasēna, and his very sight inspires them with a feeling of gratitude and confidence.

— — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — — —

*ṭankaṭīśvarī, and is described in the Mahābhārata as an extremely beautiful woman; and in fact when Kuntī and her sons see her for the first time they take her to be a Goddess, and are highly impressed with her beauty. Compare the following passages from the Mahābhārataः —*

***“ निश्चित्येत्थं हिडिम्बा सा भीमं दृष्ट्वा महाभुजम् ।  
इङ्गिताकारकुशला सोपासर्पच्छनैः शनैः ॥ २६ ॥  
विनम्यमानेव लता दिव्याभरणभूषिता ।  
शनैः शनैश्च तां भीमः समीपमुपसर्पतीम् ॥ २७ ॥  
हर्षमाणां तदाऽपश्यत् तन्वीं पीनपयोधराम् ।  
चन्द्राननां पद्मनेत्रां नीलकुञ्चितमूर्धजाम् ॥ २८ ॥  
सुभ्रूनासाक्षिकेशान्तां सुकुमारनखत्वचम् ।  
स्रग्दामपूरितशिखां सुसूक्ष्माम्बरधारिणीम् ॥ २९ ॥  
कृष्णां सुपाण्डुरैर्दन्तैर्बिम्बोष्ठीं चारुदर्शनाम् ।

Ghaṭōtkacha and Hiḍimbā followed by her dear companions, now arrive at the gate, and the former points out to the latter the great Kshattriya whom he has brought. In a moment, Hiḍimbā recognises in the person of that stranger her own dear lord Bhīmasēna; and pretending to be angry
